            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()
        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202310226')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2ec2b8c0d71046e38c9959ab3e886c23">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd534ab1f-fde3-4974-aed4-199613ec17e2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d144732862" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The practical application of hard carbon in sodium-ion batteries is limited by insufficient reversible capacity and low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which are caused by the lack of active sites and unstable electrode/electrolyte interface. Herein, a biomass-derived hard carbon material based on tea stems is proposed, which exhibits an ultrahigh ICE of 90.8%. This remarkable ICE is attributed to the presence of an inorganic-rich, thin, and robust solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. Furthermore, the material demonstrates excellent cycling stability, showing a capacity retention of 99.5% after 500 cycles at 280&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, when it works as the anode material in a sodium-ion full cell without presodiation, it reaches a high energy density of 212&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a superior stability, e.g., retaining 93.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 91.3%. The sodium storage capacity of this material is primarily attributed to a combined adsorption-intercalation/filling effect as confirmed by in situ XRD and ex situ Raman analyses. These findings make this biomass-derived hard carbon material a promising candidate for commercial application of sodium-ion batteries, achieving high performance at low cost.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310226-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The overuse of fossil fuels is a major cause to the greenhouse effect, resulting in frequent and intense extreme weather events worldwide. Therefore, the development of sustainable renewable energy is of practical importance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0001_R_d144732840e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Sci. Adv.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0003_R_d144732840e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Carbon.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) dominate the global electrochemical power supply market with their high energy density, long cycle life, and high rate performance. However, LIBs cannot fully meet the needs of large-scale electrochemical energy storage (EES) system because of the high cost caused by insufficient lithium resources. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as a promising commercial alternative to lithium-ion batteries in EES system with the advantages of low cost and abundant resources.</p>
         
         <p>Due to the large ionic radius of sodium ions, higher requirements are required for electrode materials used in SIBs. However, graphite, a common anode material in LIBs, is unfavorable for sodium storage mainly owing to the unstable sodium-graphite intercalation compounds. Alloys, carbon materials, organic compounds, and Ti-based oxides are the most common anode materials for SIBs, and among them, carbon materials have attracted the most intense interest owing to the advantageous properties of abundance, low cost and simple processing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0004_R_d144732840e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0005_R_d144732840e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to soft carbon, hard carbon exhibits high reversible capacity and low redox potential by taking advantages of its large layer spacing, abundant porosity and defects, rendering it promising as SIBs anode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0006_R_d144732840e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The precursor for preparing hard carbon (HC) includes biomass, resin and polymer precursors. Compared to resin-based and polymer-based, low-cost biomass-derived materials demonstrate excellent battery features and have a wide range of material sources, becoming the most popular materials employed by production enterprises.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0007_R_d144732840e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0009_R_d144732840e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, HCs also have obvious disadvantages, such as low initial Coulomb efficiency (ICE), poor rate performance, and low cycle stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0010_R_d144732840e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0012_R_d144732840e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Langmuir.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The irreversible sodium ending of HC in the first charge-discharge cycle and the excessive consumption of active sodium ions during the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) reduce the reversible capacity and ICE of HC anode, hindering its practical application in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0013_R_d144732840e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Power Sources.">13</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0015_R_d144732840e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The worldwide substantial consumption of tea ensures an adequate supply of waste tea products, especially team stems, as raw materials for advanced applications, e.g., sustainable energy applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0016_R_d144732840e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Cancer Res. Updates.">16</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0018_R_d144732840e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Mater. Horiz.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations (FAO), the total tea output of the world reached 6.5 million tons and China has around 47% of the world's tea production[19-22]. The new production lines for hard carbon materials are typically designed to accommodate quantities ranging from 2000 to 10000 tons,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0023_R_d144732840e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> supporting large-scale industrial production. Moreover, tea stems possess naturally porous structures with hollow pores, benefiting the storage of sodium ion. Combining the economical nature and porous structure of team stems, we therefore chose tea stems as the precursor material to prepare HC anode material for SIB in this study.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose a cost-effective method for preparing HC materials with excellent electrochemical performance. Specifically, we synthesized TS-1400-HCl using waste tea stems through a one-step, low-cost process. By subjecting TS-1400-HCl to a simple acid wash, a high ICE of 90.8% was achieved. Furthermore, the TS-1400-HCl electrode exhibited high cycle stability, with a capacity retention of 99.5% at 280&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles. In addition, the sodium-ion full cell with unpresodiated anode displayed high energy density and superior stability. The sodium storage behavior of TS-1400-HCl was investigated by in situ XRD and ex situ Raman spectroscopy, and the status of solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer was studied through XPS and TEM techniques.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310226-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202310226-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation process of the tea-stem-derived HC is illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The selection of carbonization temperature plays a crucial role in determining the structure and performance of the precursor material. The material was carbonized at three different temperatures: 1200 °C, 1400 °C, and 1600 °C to determine the optimal one and the results indicated that 1400 °C could offer material the best performance. To remove the inorganic impurity in tea stems, which could cause significant irreversible loss in capacity, the raw material was treated with HCl after carbonization at 1400 °C; the obtained product is denoted as TS-1400-HCl. Material carbonized at 1400 °C without HCl treatment (denoted as TS-1400) was also prepared for comparison. To investigate the pyrolysis behavior of TS-1400-HCl under an argon atmosphere, thermogravimetric analysis (TG) was performed. The TG curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrated that the raw material (tea stem) had a carbon yield of 20.4% and the structure of TS-1400-HCl remained stable until reaching a temperature of 900 °C.</p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the XRD patterns of TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl samples. Two obvious broad peaks near 23.5° and 43° are observed in TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl, which could be assigned to the (002) and (100) peaks of amorphous carbon materials. Compared to TS-1400, the (002) peak in the case of TS-1400-HCl is located at the lower angle, corresponding to a larger interlayer spacing. Two additional peaks at ≈32° and 45° are observed in the XRD pattern of TS-1400 but not of TS-1400-HCl, which are related to the inorganic components in raw materials, such as CaCO<sub>3</sub>, Ca<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>, etc., illustrating that the impure chemicals can be removed by treating the raw materials with HCl solution. FTIR analysis was employed to interpret the existing functional groups in TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1b</a>, peaks located at around 3016–3696 cm<sup>−1</sup>, 1632 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and 1045 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the stretching vibration of ─OH, C═C, and C─O, respectively. Compared to TS-1400, the peak intensities of ─OH, C═C, and C─O in TS-1400-HCl are significantly reduced, which is the discharge of volatile organic compounds such as CO, CO<sub>2</sub> and CH<sub>4</sub> produced by pyrolysis. In addition, the absorption peaks of C─H (3016–2808 cm<sup>−1</sup>), ─CH<sub>3</sub> (≈1450 cm<sup>−1</sup>, ≈1380 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> (1452–1320 cm<sup>−1</sup>, 880 cm<sup>−1</sup>) in TS-1400 disappear in TS-1400-HCl, indicating that the purity increased after acid washing, which is consistent well with the XRD results. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1c</a> shows the Raman spectra of TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl samples. The peak observed at approximately 1347 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the D band, which is indicative of a disordered defect structure in graphite. Conversely, the peak at around 1597 cm<sup>−1</sup> represents the G band, which signifies a crystalline graphite structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0024_R_d144732840e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Cent. Stem Cells Int.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results suggest that the TS-1400-HCl sample possesses a graphitized structure with a higher degree of disorder compared to TS-1400. To analyze the peaks in the Raman spectra, Lorentz function was employed. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio increased from 1.704 in TS-1400 to 1.845 in TS-1400-HCl, indicating an elevated level of disorder of TS-1400-HCl compared to TS-1400.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310226-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1cc3c964-a0ea-4863-b0dc-122490f9272b/adfm202310226-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1cc3c964-a0ea-4863-b0dc-122490f9272b/adfm202310226-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9b15f059-c937-4df2-bc3a-cff2a76f121f/adfm202310226-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1cc3c964-a0ea-4863-b0dc-122490f9272b/adfm202310226-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310226-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization of TS-1400-HCl and TS-1400. a) XRD patterns. b) FTIR spectra. c) Raman spectra. d) Adsorption–desorption isotherms. Pore size distribution of e) TS-1400-HCl and f) TS-1400. g,h) HRTEM images and j) elemental mapping images of TS-1400-HCl. i) HRTEM image of TS-1400.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To determine the specific surface area of TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl, adsorption and desorption isotherms based on the NL-DFT model were employed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1d</a>). The specific surface area of TS-1400-HCl was found to be significantly smaller (59.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) than that of TS-1400 (149.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>). The adsorption isotherms of both materials exhibited characteristics of type I and type II, along with H<sub>4</sub> hysteresis. These findings align with the expected adsorption behavior of micro-mesoporous materials. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1e,f</a> illustrate the pore size distribution of TS-1400-HCl and TS-1400 and the pore size distribution within micropore region is presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). The investigation indicate that both materials predominantly comprise micropores and include a limited number of medium-sized and large-sized pores. The hierarchical porous structure could promote the diffusion and adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, thereby facilitating electrolyte penetration. The above analysis primarily focuses on open pores rather than closed ones. Closed pores are crucial for effective storage of sodium in hard carbon. To detect open and closed pores simultaneously, small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) techniques was employed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0025_R_d144732840e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the nanopore region in SAXS data (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), it was found that the pore volume exhibits a wide distribution prior to acid treatment, with closed pore radii ranging from 1 to 18 Å; while after acid treatment, the radius of closed pores decreased and the distribution narrowed down to a range of 2–14 Å. Additionally, the pore volume distribution became more concentrated upon acid treatment. Combining BET and SAXS results, it is evident that the acid treatment can effectively reduce the specific surface area and pore volume of the HC material, consequently diminishing the presence of irreversible sodium storage sites on the surface of the HC material.</p>
            
            <p>High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was employed to analyze the microstructure of samples. Comparing TS-1400 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) with TS-1400-HCl (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1g,h</a>), TS-1400-HCl exhibited a higher level of porosity and a greater degree of disorder; in contrast, TS-1400 displayed long and regular lattice streaks, indicating a more ordered graphite structure. The ring-shaped pseudo-graphite structure observed in TS-1400-HCl, accompanied by numerous nanovoids, aligns well with the Raman spectroscopy results and pore size distribution analysis. According to the elemental mapping images of TS-1400-HCl (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0001">1j</a>) and TS-1400 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), metallic elements such as Mg, K, and Ca was found to be effectively removed upon HCl treatment. The small amount of N, P, and S elements detected in TS-1400-HCl should originate from tea stems (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and these elements may have a positive impact on sodium storage because of synergistic effect. TS-1400 shows an interlayer spacing of 0.376&nbsp;nm, which increased to 0.391&nbsp;nm in TS-1400-HCl after HCl treatment, consistent with the reported article.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0019_R_d144732840e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Sustainable Mater. Technol.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enlarged interlayer spacing in TS-1400-HCl could facilitate intercalated storage of Na<sup>+</sup>. Combined these analyses, it could conclude that HCl pretreatment can effectively eliminate inorganic impurities and some functional groups, and increase the abundance of smaller pores, the disorder degree and the layer spacing, offering an increasing number of active sites and migration channels for sodium ions.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202310226-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl was tested in a CR2025 half-cell system. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the first three galvanostatic discharge–charge (GCD) curves at 28&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The curves of both samples show two potential regions, the sloping area above 0.1&nbsp;V and the platform area below 0.1&nbsp;V. The first charging capacity increased from 229 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for TS-1400 to 303 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for TS-1400-HCl, while the ICE increased from 87.8% to 90.8%, superior to most of the reported carbon-based anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0026_R_d144732840e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Mater. Chem. Front.">26</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0028_R_d144732840e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the increasing ICE of TS-1400-HCl electrode may be induced by acid washing. HCl treatment enables the removal of impure ingredients in raw materials, thereby altering the microstructure of HCs such as layer spacing and active sites. More active centers contribute to the electrochemical activity of TS-1400-HCl. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0002">2b</a> further compares the rate capability of the two samples at different current densities from 28 to 560&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. As the current density increased from 28 to 560&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, both anodes exhibited capacity decay, and capacity decay was more significant at high current densities. TS-1400 showed the charge specific capacities of 239, 219, 196, 170, and 132 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 28, 56, 140, 280, and 560&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In comparison, TS-1400-HCl exhibited the charge specific capacities of 308, 283, 265, 245, and 215 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 28, 56, 140, 280, 560&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The cycle performance of TS-1400 and TS-1400-HCl are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0002">2c,d</a>, respectively. TS-1400-HCl delivered better cycle stability than that of TS-1400. When cycling at a current rate of 280&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the TS-1400-HCl displayed capacity retention of 99.5% after 500 cycles, while TS-1400 presented an initial reversible capacity of 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and fades to 73 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles with a capacity retention of 45.4%. The increase in capacity observed between 100 and 150 cycles can be attributed to the slower activation process at higher current densities. Additionally, a slight upward trend was observed after 100 cycles, indicating that the active material was fully saturated with electrolyte. TS-1400-HCl demonstrated exceptional cycling performance, showcasing excellent capacity retention that surpasses the majority of reported HC anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0002">2f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0029_R_d144732840e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - RSC Adv.">29</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0031_R_d144732840e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310226-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0617af1f-fdc9-4aa7-b7eb-bc3e1511be60/adfm202310226-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0617af1f-fdc9-4aa7-b7eb-bc3e1511be60/adfm202310226-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f167df5e-c56a-44f7-bddc-e7f29a445f29/adfm202310226-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0617af1f-fdc9-4aa7-b7eb-bc3e1511be60/adfm202310226-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310226-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202310226-sec-0050-title">2.3 Mechanism Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To study the sodium storage mechanism, the structural evolution of TS-1400-HCl during the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process was investigated by in situ XRD and ex situ Raman. In-situ XRD patterns reveal the changes in layer spacing during the charge-discharge process (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0003">3a</a>). The (002) peak intensity of the TS-1400-HCl electrode gradually decreased with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, and then gradually increases as the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. The decrease in the intensity of the (002) peak is related to disordered transitions of carbon microstructures and the presence of scattered substances between graphite-like layers caused by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. At the beginning of discharge of the TS-1400-HCl electrode, the (002) peak is located at 23.23° and the peak of the previous laps did not shift. As the degree of sodiation increased, the (002) peak underwent a slight shift towards a lower angle and eventually reached 22.03° upon complete discharge. The XRD patterns indicate that the layer spacing expands from 0.383 to 0.403&nbsp;nm during the sodiation process. During the subsequent charging process, the (002) peak gradually shifted toward a higher angle, accompanied by a decrease in the layer spacing, ultimately returning to 0.384&nbsp;nm. This observation suggests the excellent structural stability of TS-1400-HCl during the sodiation/desodiation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0003">3d</a>). Moreover, the changing trend of (002) peak in the second charge and discharge processes is consistent with that of the first cycle, indicating good reversibility during the whole charge-discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0032_R_d144732840e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0033_R_d144732840e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Energy Lett.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the in-situ XRD patterns, it is observed that the (002) peaks remained unchanged at the initial stage of discharge. However, as the discharge process progressed to a certain extent, the (002) peaks began to shift towards a lower angle. This observation suggests that the sodium-ion storage mechanism of the TS-1400-HCl electrode involves an adsorption and a subsequent interlayer insertion process. In situ XRD tests of TS-1400 were also conducted for comparison (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information) and it was found that the (002) peak of TS-1400 is weak with respect to TS-1400-HCl, indicating its low crystallinity and impure nature, which aligns with the results obtained by conventional XRD analysis of powder samples. The sodium storage mechanism of TS-1400 was found to be similar to that of TS-1400-HCl. Initially, sodium adsorption took place in the slope region, followed by interlayer intercalation, leading to a change in the layer spacing. The layer spacing was enlarged from 0.368 to 0.402&nbsp;nm upon sodiation and subsequently reverted to the original value upon denitrification.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310226-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0b86af0-1d88-4309-babb-98aa05a9a787/adfm202310226-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0b86af0-1d88-4309-babb-98aa05a9a787/adfm202310226-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/818cd665-b581-4ec5-8eca-705a6de60160/adfm202310226-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0b86af0-1d88-4309-babb-98aa05a9a787/adfm202310226-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310226-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Identifications of the sodium storage mechanism of the TS-1400-HCl. a) In situ XRD patterns during the first two cycles at 140&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and the corresponding evolution of layer spacing calculated based on the XRD patterns. b) Ex situ Raman spectra of TS-1400-HCl at various discharged states: I) pristine electrode, galvanically discharged to II) 1.0, III) 0.6, IV) 0.11, V) 0.08, VI) 0.06, VII) 0.03 and VIII) 0.01&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. c,d)The illustration of the sodiation mechanism of TS-1400-HCl.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further confirm the proposed sodium storage mechanism of the TS-1400-HCl anode, the ex-situ Raman spectra were obtained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0003">3b</a>; fitting data are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Generally, when electrons transfer to the graphite layer with the insertion of alkali metal ions, the π* anti-bonds will be occupied and the C─C bonds will be weakened and lengthened, giving rise to the redshift of the G band in Raman spectra. In addition, the adsorption of alkali metal ions at pores and defect locations inclines to limit the respiratory vibration of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon rings at the edges of the defect and carbon layer, leading to the weakening of the D band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0034_R_d144732840e887" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As sodiation proceeded, no shift in the D and G band position appeared but the ratios of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> increased from 1.164 at open-circuit voltage to 1.194 at 0.11&nbsp;V, indicating the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions at defective sites is in the slope region. In the plateau region, the D band appeared redshift, indicating significant adsorption (or filling) of Na<sup>+</sup> take place at the pores. The G band also appeared redshift and was significantly widened, which indicates the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> between graphite-like layers. The Raman results indicate that the high sodium storage capacity of TS-1400-HCl materials is the result of adsorption-intercalation/filling as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0003">3c</a>. The ex situ Raman results of TS-1400 are consistent with those of TS-1400-HCl, indicating that they share the same sodium storage mechanism (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202310226-sec-0060-title">2.4 Interfacial Chemistry Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The Coulombic efficiency and stability of the anodes are closely related to the composition of the electrode/electrolyte interface. Electrolytes can affect battery performance through their effects on solid electrolyte interfaces (SEI) and electrodes. The properties of SEI strongly influence the ICE, cycle stability, and charge transfer kinetics of the battery. Thin, stable, and robust ether-based SEI is essential for increasing ICE, accelerating interfacial charge transfer, and stabilizing electrode structure, which significantly affects the sodium storage capacity of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0035_R_d144732840e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano.">35</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0037_R_d144732840e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To elucidate the correlation between the Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage performance and the SEI properties of TS-1400-HCl anode, the composition, and structure of the SEI layer were carefully investigated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0038_R_d144732840e933" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0040_R_d144732840e933" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4a,c</a>, the C1s peaks indicate the existence of C─C and C═O groups, and the O1s peaks display C─O and C═O groups in the pristine electrode. After cycling, C1s peaks in the TS-1400-HCl electrode are dominated by C─C, C─O, C═O, and C─F groups. The content of C─F groups gradually increased from the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle to the 50<sup>th</sup> cycle, while O─F groups appear at the O1s peak. The F1s peaks display C─F groups, indicating that the electrolyte decomposition product covers the TS-1400-HCl electrode. The SEI layer of the TS-1400-HCl electrode is composed of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NaF (fitting data in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), and the F1s peak of NaF in SEI may be due to the decomposition of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> salts. These XPS results for the TS-1400-HCl electrode in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-diglyme electrolyte suggest that the decomposition products of the electrolyte are composed of C, O, and F, which dominate the inorganic SEI layer on the TS-1400-HCl electrode. In addition, the SEI composition on the TS-1400-HCl anode after three cycles was analyzed using XPS with varying etching depths (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the C content in the SEI increased as the etching depth increased, while the F content decreased. This observation suggests a significant proportion of strong polar C─F bonds on the electrode surface could serve as effective Lewis base sites for capturing and adsorbing sodium ions. Furthermore, as the etching depth increased, the SEI on the TS-1400-HCl electrode is predominantly composed of NaF, which is uniformly distributed. The high concentration of inorganic components, particularly those containing F, in the SEI is known to contribute to the improved interfacial stability and efficient transport of sodium ions at the anode/electrolyte interphase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0041_R_d144732840e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings align with the formation of a durable SEI layer and the excellent electrochemical performance, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0002">2</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310226-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/35dda50a-552d-4e01-a275-41234fa10f39/adfm202310226-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/35dda50a-552d-4e01-a275-41234fa10f39/adfm202310226-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/02ec37fb-5085-412b-9e20-d5dba1ac7f5e/adfm202310226-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/35dda50a-552d-4e01-a275-41234fa10f39/adfm202310226-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310226-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Interfacial chemistry of TS-1400-HCl. The high-resolution XPS spectra of a) C 1s, b) O 1s, and c) F 1s collected at Pristine, the TS-1400-HCl electrodes after first discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V (D-0.01&nbsp;V), after the first charge to 3.0&nbsp;V (C-3.0&nbsp;V), and after 50 cycles at 1.4 A g<sup>−1</sup> (C-50 cycle), respectively. d,e) HRTEM images of the pristine electrode. f–j) HRTEM images TS-1400-HCl at the 50<sup>th</sup> cycle at 1.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4d,e</a> shows the HRTEM images of the pristine electrode with abundant defects, micropores, and pseudo graphite domains. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4f,g</a> shows the HRTEM images of TS-1400-HCl collected at the 50<sup>th</sup> cycle. In the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>–diglyme electrolyte, a uniform and thin SEI layer with a thickness of approximately 4.43&nbsp;nm was observed on TS-1400-HCl. After 50 cycles, the interfacial layer of TS-1400-HCl exhibited a multilayer structure, consisting of an outermost SEI layer and porous pseudo-SEI layers near the surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4h</a>). The presence of a uniform and stable SEI layer results in a low R<sub>SEI</sub> value and maintains a stable pseudo graphite structure for the TS-1400-HCl electrode, consequently enhancing the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> and ensures long-term cycle stability. Notably, the locally ordered graphite layer in different HC regions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4j</a>) contrasts with the internally disordered graphite layer in TS-1400-HCl (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4i</a>). The diglyme-based electrolytes effectively modulate the interfacial SEI and pseudo-SEI layers, leading to improved interfacial microstructure and fine-tuned chemistry in the HC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0042_R_d144732840e1040" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ. Sci.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The combination of a stable layer-by-layer SEI and the presence of a “pseudo-SEI” layer facilitates the sodium storage and significantly prolongs the cycle life of the TS-1400-HCl anode in SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202310226-sec-0070-title">2.5 Dynamic Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To gain further insights into the sodium storage mechanism of the TS-1400-HCl anode, the changes in resistance and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) were investigated during the sodiation/desodiation process using electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5a</a> displays the EIS spectra of TS-1400-HCl, and the EIS spectra for TS-1400 can be found in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). The TS-1400-HCl exhibits smaller <i>R</i>-values at various cycling states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5b</a>, specific data presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the <i>R</i>-value of TS-1400-HCl does not increase as the cycling progresses. The SEI resistance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) is found to be small, and the interface charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) remains relatively unchanged after the formation of the SEI layer. This indicates that the SEI layer formed on TS-1400-HCl is thin yet robust, which aligns with the SEI characterization presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0004">4</a>. The presence of a thin and uniform SEI layer is a crucial factor enabling the TS-1400-HCl electrode to exhibit rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage kinetics and maintain long-term cycle stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310226-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d4dec8d9-7be4-48f7-bba2-6a58c51bf6bf/adfm202310226-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d4dec8d9-7be4-48f7-bba2-6a58c51bf6bf/adfm202310226-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1a3f0941-183b-48bf-8860-c15d7936c731/adfm202310226-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d4dec8d9-7be4-48f7-bba2-6a58c51bf6bf/adfm202310226-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310226-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The Nyquist plots of TS-1400-HCl electrodes before the cycle, after the 1<sup>st</sup>, 5<sup>th</sup>, 50<sup>th</sup>, and 100<sup>th</sup> cycle. b) Comparison of <i>R</i> values of TS-1400-HCl and TS-1400. c) Chemical Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients calculated based on GITT curves of TS-1400-HCl, d) CV curves of TS-1400-HCl electrodes in the first five cycles at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, e) CV curves at various scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> for TS-1400-HCl. f) Log (<i>v</i>) versus log (<i>I</i>) of TS-1400-HCl calculated based on the CV curves. g) Solvation structures of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolytes: snapshots of the MD simulation cell of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> electrolyte. h) MSD versus time plots.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The kinetic properties of sodium ions were further determined by the GITT method during the second sodiation/desodiation process with a pulse current at 280&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and rest intervals were 1&nbsp;h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). According to Fick's second law, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> is calculated by the following equation:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202310226-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/79c84f33-99e9-4570-8edf-9b03165cf831/adfm202310226-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202310226:adfm202310226-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202310226-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>D</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>Na</mi>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                 </msup>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>π</mi>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>m</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>V</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>M</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mi>S</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi>τ</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}}=\frac{4}{\pi \tau}\ {\left(\frac{{m}_{\mathrm{B}}{V}_{\mathrm{m}}}{{M}_{\mathrm{B}}S}\right)}^{2}{\left(\frac{\mathrm{\Delta}{E}_{\mathrm{s}}}{\mathrm{\Delta}{E}_{\tau}}\right)}^{2} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>τ</i> is the relaxation time, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the mass of active material, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> is the molar mass of active material, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> is the molar volume of hard carbon, <i>S</i> is the surface area of active material, <i>ΔE</i><sub>S</sub> and <i>ΔE</i><sub>t</sub> can be obtained from GITT curves. The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> is observed to be distributed within the range of 10<sup>−12.8</sup> to 10<sup>−10.8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5c</a>. This value is higher compared to that of TS-1400 (shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a,b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the acid treatment enhances the kinetics during the charge-discharge process, leading to excellent electrochemical performance.</div>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5d</a> shows the first five CV curves with a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The pair of sharp and symmetrical oxidation/reduction peaks at approximately 0.1/0.01&nbsp;V correspond to sodium ion extraction/insertion into the graphene layer, respectively. Meanwhile, the wide irreversible peak at about 0.8&nbsp;V can be attributed to the decomposition of electrolytes at the low potential surface of TS-1400-HCl followed by the formation of SEI. The attenuation of irreversible peaks in subsequent scans is a protection against the previously generated dense SEI. In addition, the scan curves of subsequent cycles overlap well, which indicates the high reversibility of the TS-1400-HCl electrode during the charge-discharge process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5e</a> shows the CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As the scan rate increased, the oxidation peak shifted slightly towards a higher potential, indicating a gentle polarization of the TS-1400-HCl electrode. The electrochemical storage process can be determined by the equation of <i>I</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constant values; <i>I</i> and <i>V</i> are the peak current and sweep rate, respectively. Typically, <i>b</i> value close to 1.0 indicates greater capacitance, while a value close to 0.5 indicates a diffusion control process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5f</a> shows that the <i>b</i> value of TS-1400-HCl is 0.33 and 0.34 for peak I and peak II, respectively, while peak I and peak II correspond to potential ranges less than 0.1&nbsp;V, indicating that the sodium storage of TS-1400-HCl in the platform region is controlled by the diffusion process. And the <i>b</i> value of TS-1400 is 0.29 and 0.42 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c,d</a>, Supporting Information), which demonstrated that the sodium storage of TS-1400 in the platform region is also controlled by the diffusion process.</p>
            
            <p>Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were conducted to illustrate the solvation structure of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> electrolyte. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5g</a>, Na<sup>+</sup> can form bonds with O<sup>2−</sup> and F<sup>−</sup>, the distance of Na─O bonds is 2.17 Å, and the distance of Na─F bonds is 2.68 Å. Based on MSD verse time plots, the diffusion coefficient could be calculated. At 298 K, the diffusion coefficient of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> is 9.72 × 10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> ps<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0005">5h</a>). The large diffusion coefficient in the electrolyte facilitates the rapid transport of sodium ions.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202310226-sec-0080-title">2.6 Electrochemical Performance of TS-1400-HCl//NVP Full Cell</h3>
            
            <p>To further assess the practical application potential of TS-1400-HCl materials, a sodium-ion full cell was assembled using a TS-1400-HCl anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode. Due to the decomposition of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> salt under high voltage, the electrolyte chosen for the full cell was 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0043_R_d144732840e1355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It was observed that the two electrolytes exhibited similar electrochemical properties in the TS-1400-HCl half-cell, and changing the electrolyte in the full cell did not affect the final conclusion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The schematic illustration of the TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6a</a>, with 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme serving as the electrolyte. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6b</a> displays the CV curves of the NVP cathode and TS-1400-HCl anode during the second cycle. In the NVP//Na half-cell, an oxidation peak of 3.4&nbsp;V and a reduction peak of 3.3&nbsp;V were observed, while in the TS-1400-HCl//Na half-cell, the oxidation peak and reduction peak were observed at 0.02 and 0.11&nbsp;V, respectively. Based on the average potential of the NVP cathode and TS-1400-HCl anode, the average voltage of the full cell is predicted as 3.38&nbsp;V. The initial charge-discharge curves of TS-1400-HCl and NVP in half cells are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6c</a>, with the ICE of TS-1400-HCl and NVP in half cells measured at 89.4% and 84.7%, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310226-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d29ef2f-9d3b-4dec-8dbf-35f7b41e21ef/adfm202310226-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d29ef2f-9d3b-4dec-8dbf-35f7b41e21ef/adfm202310226-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6ccf95ac-4d76-4c8a-bb2c-6df907442a74/adfm202310226-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d29ef2f-9d3b-4dec-8dbf-35f7b41e21ef/adfm202310226-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310226-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310226" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell. a) Schematic illustration of TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell; b) CV curves of TS-1400-HCl and NVP in half cells at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>; c) The initial charge–discharge curves of TS-1400-HCl and NVP in half cells; d) The first-two charge–discharge curves of presodiated TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell and unpresodiated TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> respectively; e) rate capability and f) cycle performance; g) the energy density versus power density plots.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As previously discussed, anode materials with ICE require a pre-sodiation process before being integrated into a full cell to reduce the loss of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte. However, the complexity and additional costs associated with pre-sodiation have limited the further development of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0044_R_d144732840e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0045_R_d144732840e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy Environ. Sci.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To assess the practicability of the designed anode, full cells with presodiated and unpresodiated TS-1400-HCl anodes were assembled, considering the electrochemical performance of the NVP cathode (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The mass ratio of TS-1400-HCl anode to NVP cathode was selected as 1.7:1, and the voltage window was set at 2.0–3.8&nbsp;V. The first two charge–discharge curves of the presodiated and unpresodiated full cells at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6d</a>. Although the presodiated TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell demonstrated a relatively higher ICE of 77.1%, the unpresodiated full cell also exhibited an acceptable ICE of 63.6%. Additionally, the unpresodiated full cell demonstrated a similar rate performance to the presodiated full cell, particularly at high rates. Specifically, the presodiated TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell achieved reversible capacities (based on cathode mass loading) of 105, 107, 107, 105, 101, and 95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6e</a>). The unpresodiated TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell demonstrated reversible capacities of 84, 103, 105, 102, 97, and 89 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Interestingly, when the current rate returned to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity of the full cell without presodiation surpassed that of the presodiated full cell. At 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, both the unpresodiated and presodiated full cells exhibit excellent cycle performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6f</a>). After 1000 cycles, the presodiated TS-1400-HCl//NVP full cell delivered a capacity of 95.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of approximately 92.4%. On the other hand, the unpresodiated full cell maintained a capacity of 93.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 91.3% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the exceptional cycle and rate performance of both types of full cells, it is evident that the full cells without presodiation exhibit similar performance to the presodiated full cells. This indicates the high feasibility of our designed TS-1400-HCl anode when applied in full cells.</p>
            
            <p>By integrating the rate capability of the full cells, the energy density versus power density plots were calculated and displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310226-fig-0006">6g</a>. The unpresodiated full-cell exhibited energy densities of 199, 212, 212, 203, 190, and 171&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at power densities of 164, 328, 654, 1622, 3218, and 6326&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>. On the other hand, the presodiated full-cell demonstrated relatively higher energy densities of 217, 219, 219, 213, 202, and 189&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at power densities of 168, 336, 669, 1666, 3310, and 6578&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>. These results clearly indicate the significant potential of TS-1400-HCl materials in practical SIBs.</p>
            
            <p>Based on the aforementioned findings, the HC material derived from HCl-treated tea stem exhibits outstanding electrochemical properties when served as the anode material in SIBs. The acid treatment plays a crucial role in the fabrication of HCs as it facilitates the formation of a hierarchical porous structure. This structure enhances electrolyte absorption, increases layer spacing, and consequently provides TS-1400-HCl electrodes with more active sites, higher specific capacity, and improved cycling performance. Furthermore, the acid treatment effectively reduces impurities and promotes a favorable electrode-interface contact, inhibiting interface side reactions and leading to the formation of a thin and stable SEI layer. The high ICE obtained through this approach has the advantage of reducing the consumption of active sodium ions from the cathode materials, ultimately contributing to the practical application of the full cell. Therefore, acid washing represents a practical method for preparing anode materials with both high ICE and long cycle stability, enabling the realization of sodium-ion full cell applications.</p>
            
            <p>Compared to high-temperature sintering (&gt;2000 °C), the low-temperature acid treatment method is more cost-effective and energy-efficient, and has been widely applied in industry due to its economic benefits. As for purification methods, one holds the potential to drive further advancements in the industry by offering improved efficiency and sustainability, such as ethanol soaking, is attractive. Tea stems are readily available, cost-effective and abundant, making them highly suitable for large-scale production of electrode materials. The primary cost is associated with the electricity consumption during the initial low-temperature acid treatment and subsequent high-temperature carbonization. The cost of preparing the TS-1400-HCl anode was estimated to be ranging from 15 000 to 25 000 Chinese yuan per ton, with a selling price of 30 000 to 45 000 yuan per ton, giving a gross profit margin of 47%. Furthermore, practical applications of TS-1400-HCl are further facilitated by its high initial coulombic efficiency (ICE), e.g., up to 90.8%, and great reversible capacity of 303 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which are comparable to commercially available products. Moreover, a button full cell was utilized to validate the practical value and the results inferred that it reaches a high energy density of 212&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a superior stability.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310226-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310226-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the porosity, degree of disorder, and layer spacing of tea stem-derived HC materials have been effectively increased through a simple acid treatment. This enhancement is beneficial for improving the sodium storage capacity. The optimized sample demonstrated a high ICE of 90.8% at 28&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and exhibited excellent long-cycle capability. Additionally, when paired with a NVP cathode, the full cell without pre-sodiation showed comparable rate performance (89 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and excellent cycling stability (93.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles) to the pre-sodiated full cells, verifying the practical feasibility of these materials in SIBs. As investigated by in situ XRD and ex-situ Raman spectroscopy, the sodium storage capacity of TS-1400-HCl was attributed to the combination of adsorption-intercalation and filling effects. The inorganic components in the SEI layer could be the major contributor to the high ICE as analyzed the composition of the SEI, and the thin and uniform SEI layer was identified as the key factor for facilitating rapid transport and ensuring long-term cycle stability within the electrode. This work is a step forward towards sustainable materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0046_R_d144732840e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Lett.">46</a>-<a href="#adfm202310226-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310226-bib-0048_R_d144732840e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The discovery of this economical and sustainable electrode material will facilitate the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries and related sustainable materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
